# IMPORTANT: Source Code Access

**Local source files are not present.** Your workspace does not contain source code. You **MUST** use Sourcegraph MCP tools to discover, read, and understand code before making any changes.

Use `sg_list_repos` to discover available repositories before searching.


## Required Workflow

1. **Search first** — Use MCP tools to find relevant files and understand existing patterns
2. **Read remotely** — Use `sg_read_file` to read full file contents from Sourcegraph
3. **Edit locally** — Use Edit, Write, and Bash to create or modify files in your working directory
4. **Verify locally** — Run tests with Bash to check your changes

## Tool Selection

| Goal | Tool |
|------|------|
| Exact symbol/string | `sg_keyword_search` |
| Concepts/semantic search | `sg_nls_search` |
| Trace usage/callers | `sg_find_references` |
| See implementation | `sg_go_to_definition` |
| Read full file | `sg_read_file` |
| Browse structure | `sg_list_files` |
| Find repos | `sg_list_repos` |
| Search commits | `sg_commit_search` |
| Track changes | `sg_diff_search` |
| Compare versions | `sg_compare_revisions` |

**Decision logic:**
1. Know the exact symbol? → `sg_keyword_search`
2. Know the concept, not the name? → `sg_nls_search`
3. Need definition of a symbol? → `sg_go_to_definition`
4. Need all callers/references? → `sg_find_references`
5. Need full file content? → `sg_read_file`

## Scoping (Always Do This)

```
repo:^github.com/ORG/REPO$           # Exact repo (preferred)
repo:github.com/ORG/                 # All repos in org
file:.*\.ts$                         # TypeScript only
file:src/api/                        # Specific directory
```

Start narrow. Expand only if results are empty.

## Efficiency Rules

- Chain searches logically: search → read → references → definition
- Don't re-search for the same pattern; use results from prior calls
- Prefer `sg_keyword_search` over `sg_nls_search` when you have exact terms
- Read 2-3 related files before synthesising, rather than one at a time
- Don't read 20+ remote files without writing code — once you understand the pattern, start implementing

## If Stuck

If MCP search returns no results:
1. Broaden the search query (synonyms, partial identifiers)
2. Try `sg_nls_search` for semantic matching
3. Use `sg_list_files` to browse the directory structure
4. Use `sg_list_repos` to verify the repository name

# Django URL Patterns Migration from patterns() to list

## Your Task

Find all Python source files in django/django that implement URL routing: the removed patterns() function (pre-1.10), the current urlpatterns list handling, the include() function, and the URL dispatcher that processes both legacy and current URL definitions.

## Context

You are working on a codebase task involving repos from the migration domain.

## Available Resources

The local `/workspace/` directory contains: sg-evals/django--674eda1c.

**Note:** Additional repositories are accessible via Sourcegraph MCP tools:
- `sg-evals/django--674eda1c` (django/django)

Perfect! I've completed a comprehensive analysis of Django's URL routing system. Here's a summary of what I found:

## Key Findings

**10 Core Files Identified:**
1. **django/urls/conf.py** - Contains `include()` function and `_path()` helper
2. **django/urls/resolvers.py** - Contains URLPattern, URLResolver, and pattern classes (RegexPattern, RoutePattern, LocalePrefixPattern)
3. **django/urls/__init__.py** - Public API exports
4. **django/urls/base.py** - `resolve()` and `reverse()` functions
5. **django/urls/converters.py** - Path parameter converters (int, str, slug, uuid, path)
6. **django/urls/exceptions.py** - Resolver404 and NoReverseMatch exceptions
7. **django/urls/utils.py** - Helper utilities (get_callable, get_mod_func)
8. **django/conf/urls/__init__.py** - Error handlers and include export
9. **django/conf/urls/i18n.py** - i18n_patterns() for multilingual URLs
10. **django/core/checks/urls.py** - URL configuration validation

**Legacy patterns() Function:**
The removed `patterns()` function (deprecated in Django 1.8, removed in 1.10) took a prefix string as the first argument and was replaced by modern `path()`/`re_path()` with `urlpatterns` as a simple list. Documentation is in `docs/releases/1.8.txt` and `docs/releases/1.10.txt`.

**URL Dispatch Chain:**
`include()` → `_path()` → `URLResolver`/`URLPattern` → `RoutePattern`/`RegexPattern` → `resolve()`/`reverse()` → `get_resolver()`

The answer has been saved to `/workspace/answer.json` with all relevant files, symbols, and detailed explanation of the Django URL routing implementation.

You can search across multiple fields in your relational\n36: database, combine the searches with other lookups, use different language\n37: configurations and weightings, and rank the results by relevance.\n38: \n39: It also now includes trigram support, using the :lookup:`trigram_similar`\n40: lookup, and the :class:`~django.contrib.postgres.search.TrigramSimilarity` and\n41: :class:`~django.contrib.postgres.search.TrigramDistance` expressions.\n42: \n43: New-style middleware\n44: --------------------\n45: \n46: :doc:`A new style of middleware is introduced </topics/http/middleware>` to\n47: solve the lack of strict request/response layering of the old-style of\n48: middleware described in `DEP 0005\n49: <https://github.com/django/deps/blob/main/final/0005-improved-middleware.rst>`_.\n50: You'll need to :ref:`adapt old, custom middleware <upgrading-middleware>` and\n51: switch from the ``MIDDLEWARE_CLASSES`` setting to the new :setting:`MIDDLEWARE`\n52: setting to take advantage of the improvements.\n53: \n54: Official support for Unicode usernames\n55: --------------------------------------\n56: \n57: The :class:`~django.contrib.auth.models.User` model in ``django.contrib.auth``\n58: originally only accepted ASCII letters and numbers in usernames. Although it\n59: wasn't a deliberate choice, Unicode characters have always been accepted when\n60: using Python 3.\n61: \n62: The username validator now explicitly accepts Unicode characters by\n63: default on Python 3 only.\n64: \n65: Custom user models may use the new\n66: :class:`~django.contrib.auth.validators.ASCIIUsernameValidator` or\n67: :class:`~django.contrib.auth.validators.UnicodeUsernameValidator`.\n68: \n69: Minor features\n70: --------------\n71: \n72: :mod:`django.contrib.admin`\n73: ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~\n74: \n75: * For sites running on a subpath, the default :attr:`URL for the \"View site\"\n76:   link <django.contrib.admin.AdminSite.site_url>` at the top of each admin page\n77:   will now point to ``request.META['SCRIPT_NAME']`` if set, instead of ``/``.\n78: \n79: * The success message that appears after adding or editing an object now\n80:   contains a link to the object's change form.\n81: \n82: * All inline JavaScript is removed so you can enable the\n83:   ``Content-Security-Policy`` HTTP header if you wish.\n84: \n85: * The new :attr:`InlineModelAdmin.classes\n86:   <django.contrib.admin.InlineModelAdmin.classes>` attribute allows specifying\n87:   classes on inline fieldsets. Inlines with a ``collapse`` class will be\n88:   initially collapsed and their header will have a small \"show\" link.\n89: \n90: * If a user doesn't have the add permission, the ``object-tools`` block on a\n91:   model's changelist will now be rendered (without the add button). This makes\n92:   it easier to add custom tools in this case.\n93: \n94: * The :class:`~django.contrib.admin.models.LogEntry` model now stores change\n95:   messages in a JSON structure so that the message can be dynamically\n96:   translated using the current active language. A new\n97:   ``LogEntry.get_change_message()`` method is now the preferred way of\n98:   retrieving the change message.\n99: \n100: * Selected objects for fields in ``ModelAdmin.raw_id_fields`` now have a link\n101:   to object's change form.\n102: \n103: * Added \"No date\" and \"Has date\" choices for ``DateFieldListFilter`` if the\n104:   field is nullable.\n105: \n106: * The jQuery library embedded in the admin is upgraded from version 2.1.4 to\n107:   2.2.3.\n108: \n109: :mod:`django.contrib.auth`\n110: ~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~~\n111: \n112: * Added support for the :ref:`Argon2 password hash <argon2_usage>`. It's\n113:   recommended over PBKDF2, however, it's not the default as it requires a\n114:   third-party library.\n115: \n116: * The default iteration count for the PBKDF2 password hasher has been increased\n117:   by 25%. This backwards compatible change will not affect users who have\n118:   subclassed ``django.contrib.auth.hashers.PBKDF2PasswordHasher`` to change the\n119:   default value.\n120: \n121: * The ``django.contrib.auth.views.logout()`` view sends \"no-cache\" headers\n122:   to prevent an issue where Safari caches redirects and prevents a user from\n123:   being able to log out.\n124: \n125: * redirect of the\n129:   ``django.contrib.auth.views.logout()`` view, if the view doesn't get a\n130:   ``next_page`` argument.\n131: \n132: * The new ``redirect_authenticated_user`` parameter for the\n133:   ``django.contrib.auth.views.login()`` view allows redirecting\n134:   authenticated users visiting the login page.\n135: \n136: * The new   "content": "{\"content\":\"1: ==============\\n2: URL dispatcher\\n3: ==============\\n4: \\n5: A clean, elegant URL scheme is an important detail in a high-quality web\\n6: application. Django lets you design URLs however you want, with no framework\\n7: limitations.\\n8: \\n9: See `Cool URIs don't change`_, by World Wide Web creator Tim Berners-Lee, for\\n10: excellent arguments on why URLs should be clean and usable.\\n11: \\n12: .. _Cool URIs don't change: https://www.w3.org/Provider/Style/URI\\n13: \\n14: Overview\\n15: ========\\n16: \\n17: To design URLs for an app, you create a Python module informally called a\\n18: **URLconf** (URL configuration). This module is pure Python code and is a\\n19: mapping between URL path expressions to Python functions (your views).\\n20: \\n21: This mapping can be as short or as long as needed. It can reference other\\n22: mappings. And, because it's pure Python code, it can be constructed\\n23: dynamically.\\n24: \\n25: Django also provides a way to translate URLs according to the active\\n26: language. See the :ref:`internationalization documentation\\n27: <url-internationalization>` for more information.\\n28: \\n29: .. _how-django-processes-a-request:\\n30: \\n31: How Django processes a request\\n32: ==============================\\n33: \\n34: When a user requests a page from your Django-powered site, this is the\\n35: algorithm the system follows to determine which Python code to execute:\\n36: \\n37: #. Django determines the root URLconf module to use. Ordinarily, this is the\\n38:    value of the :setting:`ROOT_URLCONF` setting, but if the incoming\\n39:    ``HttpRequest`` object has a :attr:`~django.http.HttpRequest.urlconf`\\n40:    attribute (set by middleware), its value will be used in place of the\\n41:    :setting:`ROOT_URLCONF` setting.\\n42: \\n43: #. Django loads that Python module and looks for the variable\\n44:    ``urlpatterns``. This should be a :term:`sequence` of\\n45:    :func:`django.urls.path` and/or :func:`django.urls.re_path` instances.\\n46: \\n47: #. Django runs through each URL pattern, in order, and stops at the first\\n48:    one that matches the requested URL, matching against\\n49:    :attr:`~django.http.HttpRequest.path_info`.\\n50: \\n51: #. Once one of the URL patterns matches, Django imports and calls the given\\n52:    view, which is a Python function (or a :doc:`class-based view\\n53:    </topics/class-based-views/index>`). The view gets passed the following\\n54:    arguments:\\n55: \\n56:    * An instance of :class:`~django.http.HttpRequest`.\\n57:    * If the matched URL pattern contained no named groups, then the\\n58:      matches from the regular expression are provided as positional arguments.\\n59:    * The keyword arguments are made up of any named parts matched by the\\n60:      path expression that are provided, overridden by any arguments specified\\n61:      in the optional ``kwargs`` argument to :func:`django.urls.path` or\\n62:      :func:`django.urls.re_path`.\\n63: \\n64: #. If no URL pattern matches, or if an exception is raised during any\\n65:    point in this process, Django invokes an appropriate\\n66:    error-handling view. See `Error handling`_ below.\\n67: \\n68: Example\\n69: =======\\n70: \\n71: Here's a sample URLconf::\\n72: \\n73:     from django.urls import path\\n74: \\n75:     from . import views\\n76: \\n77:     urlpatterns = [\\n78:         path(\\\"articles/2003/\\\", views.special_case_2003),\\n79:         path(\\\"articles/<int:year>/\\\", views.year_archive),\\n80:         path(\\\"articles/<int:year>/<int:month>/\\\", views.month_archive),\\n81:         path(\\\"articles/<int:year>/<int:month>/<slug:slug>/\\\", views.article_detail),\\n82:     ]\\n83: \\n84: Notes:\\n85: \\n86: * To capture a value from the URL, use angle brackets.\\n87: \\n88: * Captured values can optionally include a converter type. For example, use\\n89:   ``<int:name>`` to capture an integer parameter. If a converter isn't\\n90:   included, any string, excluding a ``/`` character, is matched.\\n91: \\n92: * There's no need to add a leading slash, because every URL has that. For\\n93:   example, it's ``articles``, not ``/articles``.\\n94: \\n95: Example requests:\\n96: \\n97: * A request to ``/articles/2005/03/`` would match the third entry in the\\n98:   list. Django would call the function\\n99:   ``views.month_archive(request, year=2005, month=3)``.\\n100: \\n101: * ``/articles/2003/`` would match the first pattern in the list, not the\\n102:   second one, because the patterns are tested in order, and the first one\\n103:   is the first test to pass. Feel free to exploit the ordering to insert\\n104:   special cases like this. Here, Django would call the function\\n105:   ``views.special_case_2003(request)``\\n106: \\n107: * ``/articles/2003`` would not match any of these patterns, because each\\n108:   pattern requires that the URL end with a slash.\\n109: \\n110: * ``/articles/2003/03/building-a-django-site/`` would match the final pattern.\\n111:   Django would call the function\\n112:   ``views.article_detail(request, year=2003, month=3, slug=\\\"building-a-django-site\\\")``.\\n113: \\n114: Path converters\\n115: ===============\\n116: \\n117: The following path converters are available by default:\\n118: \\n119: * ``str`` - Matches any non-empty string, excluding the path separator,\\n120:   ``'/'``. This is the default if a converter isn't included in the expression.\\n121: \\n122: * ``int`` - Matches zero or any positive integer. Returns an ``int``.\\n123: \\n124: * ``slug`` - Matches any slug string consisting of ASCII letters or numbers,\\n125:   plus the hyphen and underscore characters. For example,\\n126:   ``building-your-1st-django-site``.\\n127: \\n128: * ``uuid`` - Matches a formatted UUID. To prevent multiple URLs from mapping to\\n129:   the same page, dashes must be included and letters must be lowercase. For\\n130:   example, ``075194d3-6885-417e-a8a8-6c931e272f00``. Returns a\\n131:   :class:`~uuid.UUID` instance.\\n132: \\n133: * ``path`` - Matches any non-empty string, including the path separator,\\n134:   ``'/'``. This allows you to match against a complete URL path rather than\\n135:   a segment of a URL path as with ``str``.\\n136: \\n137: .. _registering-custom-path-converters:\\n138: \\n139: Registering custom path converters\\n140: ==================================\\n141: \\n142: For more complex matching requirements, you can define your own path\\n143: converters.\\n144: \\n145: A converter is a class that includes the following:\\n146: \\n147: * A ``regex`` class attribute, as a string.\\n148: \\n149: * A ``to_python(self, value)`` method, which handles converting the matched\\n150:   string into the type that should be passed to the view function.   "structuredContent": {
    "content": "1: ==============\n2: URL dispatcher\n3: ==============\n4: \n5: A clean, elegant URL scheme is an important detail in a high-quality web\n6: application. Django lets you design URLs however you want, with no framework\n7: limitations.\n8: \n9: See `Cool URIs don't change`_, by World Wide Web creator Tim Berners-Lee, for\n10: excellent arguments on why URLs should be clean and usable.\n11: \n12: .. _Cool URIs don't change: https://www.w3.org/Provider/Style/URI\n13: \n14: Overview\n15: ========\n16: \n17: To design URLs for an app, you create a Python module informally called a\n18: **URLconf** (URL configuration). This module is pure Python code and is a\n19: mapping between URL path expressions to Python functions (your views).\n20: \n21: This mapping can be as short or as long as needed. It can reference other\n22: mappings. And, because it's pure Python code, it can be constructed\n23: dynamically.\n24: \n25: Django also provides a way to translate URLs according to the active\n26: language. See the :ref:`internationalization documentation\n27: <url-internationalization>` for more information.\n28: \n29: .. _how-django-processes-a-request:\n30: \n31: How Django processes a request\n32: ==============================\n33: \n34: When a user requests a page from your Django-powered site, this is the\n35: algorithm the system follows to determine which Python code to execute:\n36: \n37: #. Django determines the root URLconf module to use. Ordinarily, this is the\n38:    value of the :setting:`ROOT_URLCONF` setting, but if the incoming\n39:    ``HttpRequest`` object has a :attr:`~django.http.HttpRequest.urlconf`\n40:    attribute (set by middleware), its value will be used in place of the\n41:    :setting:`ROOT_URLCONF` setting.\n42: \n43: #. Django loads that Python module and looks for the variable\n44:    ``urlpatterns``. This should be a :term:`sequence` of\n45:    :func:`django.urls.path` and/or :func:`django.urls.re_path` instances.\n46: \n47: #. Django runs through each URL pattern, in order, and stops at the first\n48:    one that matches the requested URL, matching against\n49:    :attr:`~django.http.HttpRequest.path_info`.\n50: \n51: #. Once one of the URL patterns matches, Django imports and calls the given\n52:    view, which is a Python function (or a :doc:`class-based view\n53:    </topics/class-based-views/index>`). The view gets passed the following\n54:    arguments:\n55: \n56:    * An instance of :class:`~django.http.HttpRequest`.\n57:    * If the matched URL pattern contained no named groups, then the\n58:      matches from the regular expression are provided as positional arguments.\n59:    * The keyword arguments are made up of any named parts matched by the\n60:      path expression that are provided, overridden by any arguments specified\n61:      in the optional ``kwargs`` argument to :func:`django.urls.path` or\n62:      :func:`django.urls.re_path`.\n63: \n64: #. If no URL pattern matches, or if an exception is raised during any\n65:    point in this process, Django invokes an appropriate\n66:    error-handling view. See `Error handling`_ below.\n67: \n68: Example\n69: =======\n70: \n71: Here's a sample URLconf::\n72: \n73:     from django.urls import path\n74: \n75:     from . import views\n76: \n77:     urlpatterns = [\n78:         path(\"articles/2003/\", views.special_case_2003),\n79:         path(\"articles/<int:year>/\", views.year_archive),\n80:         path(\"articles/<int:year>/<int:month>/\", views.month_archive),\n81:         path(\"articles/<int:year>/<int:month>/<slug:slug>/\", views.article_detail),\n82:     ]\n83: \n84: Notes:\n85: \n86: * To capture a value from the URL, use angle brackets.\n87: \n88: * Captured values can optionally include a converter type. For example, use\n89:   ``<int:name>`` to capture an integer parameter. If a converter isn't\n90:   included, any string, excluding a ``/`` character, is matched.\n91: \n92: * There's no need to add a leading slash, because every URL has that. For\n93:   example, it's ``articles``, not ``/articles``.\n94: \n95: Example requests:\n96: \n97: * A request to ``/articles/2005/03/`` would match the third entry in the\n98:   list. Django would call the function\n99:   ``views.month_archive(request, year=2005, month=3)``.\n100: \n101: * ``/articles/2003/`` would match the first pattern in the list, not the\n102:   second one, because the patterns are tested in order, and the first one\n103:   is the first test to pass. Feel free to exploit the ordering to insert\n104:   special cases like this. Here, Django would call the function\n105:   ``views.special_case_2003(request)``\n106: \n107: * ``/articles/2003`` would not match any of these patterns, because each\n108:   pattern requires that the URL end with a slash.\n109: \n110: * ``/articles/2003/03/building-a-django-site/`` would match the final pattern.\n111:   Django would call the function\n112:   ``views.article_detail(request, year=2003, month=3, slug=\"building-a-django-site\")``.\n113: \n114: Path converters\n115: ===============\n116: \n117: The following path converters are available by default:\n118: \n119: * ``str`` - Matches any non-empty string, excluding the path separator,\n120:   ``'/'``. This is the default if a converter isn't included in the expression.\n121: \n122: * ``int`` - Matches zero or any positive integer. Returns an ``int``.\n123: \n124: * ``slug`` - Matches any slug string consisting of ASCII letters or numbers,\n125:   plus the hyphen and underscore characters. For example,\n126:   ``building-your-1st-django-site``.\n127: \n128: * ``uuid`` - Matches a formatted UUID. To prevent multiple URLs from mapping to\n129:   the same page, dashes must be included and letters must be lowercase. For\n130:   example, ``075194d3-6885-417e-a8a8-6c931e272f00``. Returns a\n131:   :class:`~uuid.UUID` instance.\n132: \n133: * ``path`` - Matches any non-empty string, including the path separator,\n134:   ``'/'``. This allows you to match against a complete URL path rather than\n135:   a segment of a URL path as with ``str``.\n136: \n137: .. _registering-custom-path-converters:\n138: \n139: Registering custom path converters\n140: ==================================\n141: \n142: For more complex matching requirements, you can define your own path\n143: converters.\n144: \n145: A converter is a class that includes the following:\n146: \n147: * A ``regex`` class attribute, as a string.\n148: \n149: * A ``to_python(self, value)`` method, which handles converting the matched\n150:   string into the type that should be passed to the view function.
